(29,869&#160;mi)</td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background: #ddf;">Flyby of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a></th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Closest approach</th><td>March 16, 1975</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Distance</th><td>327 kilometers (203&#160;mi)</td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">\n</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><div class="" style="width:100%; text-align:center; border-top:1px #aaa solid"><b><a href="/wiki/Mariner_program" title="Mariner program">Mariner program</a></b></div><div style="width:100%"><div class="noprint" style="float: left; text-align:left;padding-right:0.5em;">&#8592;&#160;<i><a href="/wiki/Mariner_9" title="Mariner 9">Mariner 9</a></i></div></div>&#160;</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><i><b>Mariner 10</b></i> was an American <a href="/wiki/Robotic_spacecraft" title="Robotic spacecraft">robotic</a> <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probe</a> launched by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> on November 3, 1973, to fly by the planets <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>.\n</p><p><i>Mariner 10</i> was launched approximately two years after <i><a href="/wiki/Mariner_9" title="Mariner 9">Mariner 9</a></i> and was the last spacecraft in the <a href="/wiki/Mariner_program" title="Mariner program">Mariner program</a>. (Mariner 11 and 12 were allocated to the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager program</a> and redesignated <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i>.)\n</p><p>The mission objectives were to measure Mercury\'s environment, atmosphere, surface, and body characteristics and to make similar investigations of Venus. Secondary objectives were to perform experiments in the <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_medium" title="Interplanetary medium">interplanetary medium</a> and to obtain experience with a dual-planet <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assist</a> mission.  <i>Mariner 10</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s science team was led by <a href="/wiki/Bruce_C._Murray" title="Bruce C. Murray">Bruce C. Murray</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">Jet Propulsion Laboratory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schudel_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schudel-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#Departing_the_Earth&#8211;Moon_system"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Departing the Earth&#8211;Moon system</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Cruise_to_Venus"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Cruise to Venus</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#Venus_flyby"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Venus flyby</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#First_Mercury_flyby"><span flyby</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#End_of_mission"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">End of mission</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">13.1</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Bibliography_and_Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">13.2</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography and Further reading</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design_and_trajectory">Design and trajectory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mariner_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Design and trajectory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mariner_10_gravitational_slingshot.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2319" data-file-width="3504" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Mariner_10_gravitational_slingshot.jpg/220px-Mariner_10_gravitational_slingshot.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Mariner_10_gravitational_slingshot.jpg/330px-Mariner_10_gravitational_slingshot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Mariner_10_gravitational_slingshot.jpg/440px-Mariner_10_gravitational_slingshot.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mariner_10_gravitational_slingshot.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artists\' impression of the <i>Mariner 10</i> mission. The first mission to perform a <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assist</a>, it used a flyby of the planet <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> in order to decrease its perihelion. This would allow the spacecraft to meet Mercury on three occasions in 1974 and 1975.</div></div></div>\n<p><i>Mariner 10</i> was the first spacecraft to make use of an interplanetary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_slingshot" title="Gravitational slingshot">gravitational slingshot</a> maneuver, using Venus to bend its flight path and bring its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a> down to the level of Mercury\'s orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mission_Overview_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mission_Overview-3">[3]</a></sup>  This maneuver, inspired by the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_mechanics" title="Orbital mechanics">orbital mechanics</a> calculations of the Italian scientist <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Colombo" title="Giuseppe Colombo">Giuseppe Colombo</a>, put the spacecraft into an orbit that repeatedly brought it back to Mercury. <i>Mariner 10</i> used the solar <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pressure" title="Radiation pressure">radiation pressure</a> on its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_module" title="Photovoltaic module">solar panels</a> and its high-gain antenna as a means of <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">attitude control</a> during flight, the first spacecraft to use active solar pressure control.\n</p><p>The components on <i>Mariner 10</i> can be categorized into four groups based on their common function. The solar panels, power subsystem, attitude control subsystem, and computer kept the spacecraft operating properly during the flight. The navigational system, including the hydrazine rocket, would keep <i>Mariner 10</i> on track to Venus and Mercury. Several scientific instruments would collect data at the two planets. Finally, the antennas would transmit this data to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Space_Network" title="Deep Space Network">Deep Space Network</a> back on Earth, as well as receive commands from Mission Control. <i>Mariner 10</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s various components and scientific instruments were attached to a central hub, which was roughly the shape of an octagonal prism. The hub stored the spacecraft\'s internal electronics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDC_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDC-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strom16_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strom16-5">[5]</a></sup> The Mariner 10 spacecraft was manufactured by Boeing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mariner_10_Quicklook_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mariner_10_Quicklook-6">[6]</a></sup> NASA set a strict limit of $98 million for Mariner 10\'s total cost, which marked the first time the agency subjected a mission to an inflexible budget constraint. No overruns would be tolerated, so mission planners carefully considered cost efficiency when designing the spacecraft\'s instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Cost control was primarily accomplished by executing contract work closer to the launch date than was recommended by normal mission schedules, as reducing the length of available work time increased cost efficiency. Despite the rushed schedule, very few deadlines were missed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biggs_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Biggs-8">[8]</a></sup> The mission ended up about $1 million under budget.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">Attitude control</a> is needed to keep a spacecraft&#8217;s instruments and antennas aimed in the correct direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> During course maneuvers, a spacecraft may need to rotate so that its rocket faces the proper direction before being fired. <i>Mariner 10</i> determined its attitude using two optical sensors, one pointed at the Sun, and the other at a bright star, usually Canopus; additionally, the probe\'s three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gyroscopes" title="Gyroscopes">gyroscopes</a> provided a second option for calculating the attitude. Nitrogen gas thrusters were used to adjust <i>Mariner 10</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s orientation along three axes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The spacecraft&#8217;s electronics were intricate and complex: it contained over 32,000 pieces of circuitry, of which resistors, capacitors, diodes, microcircuits, and transistors were the most common devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paul_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paul-14">[14]</a></sup> Commands for the instruments could be stored on <i>Mariner 10</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s computer, but were limited to 512 words. The rest had to be broadcast by the Mission Sequence Working Group from Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shirley_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shirley-15">[15]</a></sup> Supplying the spacecraft components with power required modifying the electrical output of the solar panels. The power subsystem used two redundant sets of circuitry, each containing a booster regulator and an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inverter" title="Inverter">inverter</a>, to convert the panels\' <a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">DC</a> output to <a href="/wiki/Alternating_current" title="Alternating current">AC</a> and alter the voltage to the necessary level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The subsystem could store up to 20 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ampere_hours" title="Ampere hours">ampere hours</a> of electricity on a 39 volt <a href="/wiki/Nickel%E2%80%93cadmium_battery" title="Nickel&#8211;cadmium battery">nickel-cadmium battery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33-657_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-33-657-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The flyby past Mercury posed major technical challenges for scientists to overcome. Due to Mercury\'s proximity to the Sun, <i>Mariner 10</i> would have to endure 4.5 times more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a> than when it departed Earth&#8212;compared to previous Mariner missions, spacecraft parts needed extra shielding against the heat. Thermal blankets and a sunshade were installed on the main body.  After evaluating different choices for the sunshade cloth material, mission planners chose <a href="/wiki/Beta_cloth" title="Beta cloth">beta cloth</a>, a combination of aluminized <a href="/wiki/Kapton" title="Kapton">Kapton</a> and glass-fiber sheets treated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teflon" title="Teflon">Teflon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> However, solar shielding was unfeasible for some of <i>Mariner 10</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s other components. <i>Mariner 10</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s two solar panels needed to be kept under 115&#160;&#176;C. Covering the panels would defeat their purpose of producing electricity. The solution was to add an adjustable tilt to the panels, so the angle at which they faced the sun could be changed. Engineers considered folding the panels toward each other, making a V-shape with the main body, but tests found this approach had the potential to overheat the rest of the spacecraft. The alternative chosen was to mount the solar panels in a line and tilt them along that axis, which had the added benefit of increasing the efficiency of the spacecraft&#8217;s nitrogen jet thrusters, which could now be placed on the panel tips. The panels could be rotated a maximum of 76 degrees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strom16_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Strom16-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Additionally, <i>Mariner 10</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s hydrazine rocket nozzle had to face the Sun to function properly, but scientists rejected covering the nozzle with a thermal door as an undependable solution. Instead, a special paint was applied to exposed parts on the rocket so as to reduce heat flow from the nozzle to the delicate instruments on the spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Accurately performing the gravity assist at Venus posed another hurdle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> If <i>Mariner 10</i> was to maintain a course to Mercury, its trajectory could deviate no more than 200 kilometres (120&#160;mi) from a critical point in the vicinity of Venus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> To ensure that the necessary course corrections could be made, mission planners tripled the amount of <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">hydrazine</a> fuel Mariner 10 would carry, and also equipped the spacecraft with more nitrogen gas for the thrusters than the previous Mariner mission had held. These upgrades proved crucial in enabling the second and third Mercury flybys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MB25_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-MB25-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The mission still lacked the ultimate safeguard: a sister spacecraft. It was common for probes to be launched in pairs, with complete redundancy to guard against the failure of one or the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The budget constraint ruled this option out. Even though mission planners stayed sufficiently under budget to divert some funding for constructing a backup spacecraft, the budget did not permit both to be launched at the same time. In the event that Mariner 10 failed, NASA would only allow the backup to be launched if the fatal error was diagnosed and fixed&#8212;this would have to be completed in the two-and-a-half weeks between the scheduled launch on November 3 and the last possible launch date of November 21.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MB25_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-MB25-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MB38_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-MB38-25">[25]</a></sup> (The unused backup was sent to the Smithsonian museum)\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Instruments">Instruments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mariner_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Instruments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mariner10.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1642" data-file-width="2178" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Mariner10.jpg/220px-Mariner10.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Mariner10.jpg/330px-Mariner10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Mariner10.jpg/440px-Mariner10.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mariner10.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An illustration showing <i>Mariner 10</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s instruments</div></div></div>\n<p><i>Mariner 10</i> conducted seven experiments at Venus and Mercury. Six of these experiments had a dedicated scientific instrument to collect data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The experiments and instruments were designed by research laboratories and educational institutions from across the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Out of forty-six submissions, JPL selected seven experiments on the basis of maximizing science return without exceeding cost guidelines: together, the seven scientific experiments cost 12.6 million dollars, about one-eighth of the total mission budget.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biggs_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Biggs-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Television_photography">Television photography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mariner_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Television photography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The imaging system, the Television Photography Experiment, consisted of two 15&#160;cm (5.9&#8243;) <a href="/wiki/Cassegrain_reflector" title="Cassegrain reflector">Cassegrain</a> telescopes feeding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vidicon" title="Vidicon">vidicon</a> tubes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nssdccam_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-nssdccam-28">[28]</a></sup> The main telescope could be bypassed to a smaller wide angle optic, but using the same tube.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nssdccam_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-nssdccam-28">[28]</a></sup> It had an 8-position filter wheel, with one position occupied by a mirror for the wide-angle bypass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nssdccam_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-nssdccam-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The entire imaging system was imperiled when electric heaters attached to the cameras failed to turn on immediately after launch. To avoid the Sun\'s damaging heat, the cameras were deliberately placed on the spacecraft side facing away from the Sun. Consequently, the heaters were needed to prevent the cameras from losing heat and become so cold that they would become damaged. JPL engineers found that the vidicons could generate enough heat through normal operation to stay just above the critical temperature of -40&#160;&#176;C; therefore they advised against turning off the cameras during the flight. Test photos of the Earth and Moon showed that image quality had not been significantly affected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The mission team was pleasantly surprised when the camera heaters started working on January 17, two months after launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-No._14_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-No._14-31">[31]</a></sup> Later investigation concluded that a short circuit in a different location on the probe had prevented the heater from turning on. This allowed the vidicons to be turned off as needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Of the six main scientific instruments, the 43.6 kilograms (96&#160;lb) cameras were by far the most massive device. Requiring 67 watts of electricity, the cameras consumed more power than the other five instruments combined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_Instrument_Survey_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science_Instrument_Survey-33">[33]</a></sup> The system returned about 7000 photographs of Mercury and Venus during Mariner 10\'s flybys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nssdccam_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-nssdccam-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrared_radiometer">Infrared radiometer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mariner_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Infrared radiometer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The infrared <a href="/wiki/Radiometer" title="Radiometer">radiometer</a> detected <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared radiation</a> given off by Mercury\'s surface and Venus\' atmosphere, from which the temperature could be calculated. How quickly the surface lost heat as it rotated into the planet\'s dark side revealed aspects about the surface\'s composition, such as whether it was made out of rocks, or out of finer particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strom-18-19_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strom-18-19-35">[35]</a></sup> The infrared radiometer contained a pair of Cassegrain telescopes fixed at an angle of 120 degrees to each other, and a pair of detectors made from antimony-bismuth <a href="/wiki/Thermopile" title="Thermopile">thermopiles</a>. The instrument was designed to measure temperatures as cold as -193&#160;&#176;C and as hot as 427&#160;&#176;C. Stillman C. Chase, Jr. of the Santa Barbara Research Center headed the infrared radiometer experiment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_Instrument_Survey_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Science_Instrument_Survey-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ultraviolet_spectrometers">Ultraviolet spectrometers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mariner_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ultraviolet spectrometers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Two ultraviolet <a href="/wiki/Optical_spectrometer" title="Optical spectrometer">spectrometers</a> were involved in this experiment, one to measure UV absorption, the other to sense UV emissions. The occultation spectrometer scanned Mercury\'s edge as it passed in front of the Sun, and detected whether solar ultraviolet radiation was absorbed at certain wavelengths, which would indicate the presence of gas particles, and therefore an atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Airglow" title="Airglow">airglow</a> spectrometer detected extreme ultraviolet radiation emanating from atoms of gaseous hydrogen, helium, carbon, oxygen, neon, and argon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_Instrument_Survey_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-Science_Instrument_Survey-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Unlike the occultation spectrometer, it did not require backlighting from the Sun, and could move along with the rotatable scan platform on the spacecraft. The experiment\'s most important goal was to ascertain whether Mercury had an atmosphere, but would also gather data at Earth and Venus and study the interstellar background radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strom-18-19_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Strom-18-19-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plasma_detectors">Plasma detectors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mariner_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Plasma detectors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The plasma experiment\'s goal was to study the ionized gases (<a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a>) of the solar wind, the temperature and density of its electrons, and how the planets affected the velocity of the plasma stream.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The experiment contained two components, facing in opposite directions. The Scanning Electrostatic Analyzer was aimed toward the Sun, and could detect positive ions and electrons, which were separated by a set of three concentric hemispherical plates. The Scanning Electron Spectrometer was aimed away from the Sun, and detected only electrons, using just one hemispherical plate. The instruments could be rotated about 60 degrees to either side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_Instrument_Survey_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-Science_Instrument_Survey-33">[33]</a></sup> By gathering data on the solar wind\'s movement around Mercury, the plasma experiment could be used to verify the magnetometer\'s observations of Mercury\'s magnetic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strom-18-19_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Strom-18-19-35">[35]</a></sup> Using the plasma detectors, Mariner 10 gathered the first <i>in situ</i> solar wind data from inside Venus\' orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Shortly after launch, scientists found that the Scanning Electrostatic Analyzer had failed because a door shielding the analyzer did not open. An unsuccessful attempt was made to forcibly unfasten the door with the first course correction maneuver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-No._7_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-No._7-40">[40]</a></sup> The experiment operators had planned to observe the directions taken by positive ions prior to the ions\' collision with the Analyzer, but this data was lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-No._15_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-No._15-41">[41]</a></sup> The experiment was still able to collect some data using the properly functioning Scanning Electron Spectrometer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Charged_particle_telescopes">Charged particle telescopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mariner_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Charged particle telescopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The goal of the charged particles experiment was to observe how the <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">heliosphere</a> interacted with <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic radiation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strom_and_Sprague_2003,_pp._19_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strom_and_Sprague_2003,_pp._19-43">[43]</a></sup> In connection with the plasma detectors and magnetometers, this experiment had the potential to provide additional evidence of a magnetic field around Mercury,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> by showing whether such a field had captured charged particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_Instrument_Survey_33-4"><a href="#cite_note-Science_Instrument_Survey-33">[33]</a></sup> Two telescopes were used to collect highly energetic electrons and atomic nuclei, specifically oxygen nuclei or less massive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunne_and_Burgess_1978,_pp._24_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dunne_and_Burgess_1978,_pp._24-45">[45]</a></sup> These particles then passed through a set of detectors and were counted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_Instrument_Survey_33-5"><a href="#cite_note-Science_Instrument_Survey-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetometers">Magnetometers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mariner_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Magnetometers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Two fluxgate magnetometers were entrusted with discerning whether Mercury produced a <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetic field</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> and studying the interplanetary magnetic field between flybys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunne_and_Burgess_1978,_pp._24_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dunne_and_Burgess_1978,_pp._24-45">[45]</a></sup> In designing this experiment, scientists had to account for interference from the magnetic field generated by Mariner 10&#8217;s many electronic parts. For this reason, the magnetometers had to be situated on a long boom, one closer to the octagonal hub, the other one further away. Data from the two magnetometers would be cross-referenced to filter out the spacecraft&#8217;s own magnetic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strom_and_Sprague_2003,_pp._19_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Strom_and_Sprague_2003,_pp._19-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Drastically weakening the probe&#8217;s magnetic field would have increased costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shirley_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shirley-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Celestial_Mechanics_and_Radio_Science_experiment">Celestial Mechanics and Radio Science experiment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mariner_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Celestial Mechanics and Radio Science experiment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>This experiment investigated the mass and gravitational characteristics of Mercury. It was of particular interest because of the planet\'s closeness to the Sun, large orbital eccentricity, and unusual spin-orbit resonance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAHistory_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAHistory-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As the spacecraft passed behind Mercury on the first encounter there was an opportunity to probe the atmosphere and to measure the radius of the planet. By observing phase changes in the S-band radio signal, measurements of the atmosphere could be made. The atmosphere was assessed as having a density of about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7016100000000000000&#9824;</span>10<sup>16</sup>&#160;molecules per cm<sup>3</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAHistory_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASAHistory-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Departing_the_Earth.E2.80.93Moon_system"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Departing_the_Earth&#8211;Moon_system">Departing the Earth&#8211;Moon system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mariner_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Departing the Earth&#8211;Moon system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earth_%26_Moon_by_Mariner_10.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="516" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Earth_%26_Moon_by_Mariner_10.jpg/220px-Earth_%26_Moon_by_Mariner_10.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Earth_%26_Moon_by_Mariner_10.jpg/330px-Earth_%26_Moon_by_Mariner_10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Earth_%26_Moon_by_Mariner_10.jpg/440px-Earth_%26_Moon_by_Mariner_10.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Earth_%26_Moon_by_Mariner_10.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Mariner 10</i> imaged the Earth and Moon shortly after launch</div></div></div>\n<p>Boeing finished building the spacecraft at the end of June 1973, and <i>Mariner 10</i> was delivered from Seattle to JPL\'s headquarters in California, where JPL comprehensively tested the integrity of the spacecraft and its instruments. After the tests were finished, the probe was transported to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Test_Range" title="Eastern Test Range">Eastern Test Range</a> in Florida, the launch site. Technicians filled a tank on the spacecraft with 29 kilograms (64&#160;lb) of hydrazine fuel so that the probe could make course corrections, and attached <a href="/wiki/Squib_(explosive)" title="Squib (explosive)">squibs</a>, whose detonation would signal <i>Mariner 10</i> to exit the launch rocket and deploy its instruments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The planned gravity assist at Venus made it feasible to use an <a href="/wiki/Atlas-Centaur" title="Atlas-Centaur">Atlas-Centaur</a> rocket instead of a more powerful but more expensive <a href="/wiki/Titan_IIIC" title="Titan IIIC">Titan IIIC</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paul_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Paul-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strom,14_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strom,14-51">[51]</a></sup> The probe and the Atlas-Centaur were attached together ten days prior to liftoff. Launch posed one of the largest risks of failure for the <i>Mariner 10</i> mission&#8212;<a href="/wiki/Mariner_1" title="Mariner 1">Mariner 1</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mariner_3" title="Mariner 3">Mariner 3</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mariner_8" title="Mariner 8">Mariner 8</a> all failed minutes after lift-off due to either engineering failures or Atlas rocket malfunctions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MB38_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-MB38-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The mission had a launch period of about a month in length, from October 16, 1973, to November 21. NASA chose November 3 as the launch date because it would optimize imaging conditions when the spacecraft arrived at Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strom,14_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Strom,14-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atlas-Centaur_launch_with_Mariner_10.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2089" decoding="async" height="287" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Atlas-Centaur_launch_with_Mariner_10.jpg/200px-Atlas-Centaur_launch_with_Mariner_10.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Atlas-Centaur_launch_with_Mariner_10.jpg/300px-Atlas-Centaur_launch_with_Mariner_10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Atlas-Centaur_launch_with_Mariner_10.jpg/400px-Atlas-Centaur_launch_with_Mariner_10.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atlas-Centaur_launch_with_Mariner_10.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Launch of <i>Mariner 10</i></div></div></div>\n<p>On November 3 at 12:45 am Eastern Time, the Atlas-Centaur carrying <i>Mariner 10</i> lifted off from pad <a href="/wiki/Spaceport_Florida_Launch_Complex_36" title="Spaceport Florida Launch Complex 36">SLC-36B</a>. The Atlas stage burned for around four minutes, after which it was jettisoned, and the Centaur stage took over for an additional five minutes, propelling <i>Mariner 10</i> to a <a href="/wiki/Parking_orbit" title="Parking orbit">parking orbit</a>. The temporary orbit took the spacecraft one-third of the distance around Earth: this maneuver was needed to reach the correct spot for a second burn by the Centaur engines, which set <i>Mariner 10</i> on a path towards Venus. The probe then separated from the rocket; subsequently, the Centaur stage diverted away to avoid the possibility of future collision. Never before had a planetary mission depended upon two separate rocket burns during the launch, and even with <i>Mariner 10</i>, scientists initially viewed the maneuver as too risky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During its first week of flight, the <i>Mariner 10</i> camera system was tested by taking five <a href="/wiki/Panoramic_photography" title="Panoramic photography">photographic mosaics</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> and six of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. It also obtained photographs of the north polar region of the Moon where prior coverage was poor. These photographs provided a basis for cartographers to update lunar maps and improve the <a href="/wiki/Selenographic_coordinates" title="Selenographic coordinates">lunar control net</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cruise_to_Venus">Cruise to Venus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mariner_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Cruise to Venus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mariner-10-Trajectory-first_half.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="883" data-file-width="1211" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Mariner-10-Trajectory-first_half.PNG/220px-Mariner-10-Trajectory-first_half.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Mariner-10-Trajectory-first_half.PNG/330px-Mariner-10-Trajectory-first_half.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Mariner-10-Trajectory-first_half.PNG/440px-Mariner-10-Trajectory-first_half.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mariner-10-Trajectory-first_half.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Trajectory of <i>Mariner 10</i> spacecraft: since launch on November 3, 1973, to first fly-by of Mercury on March 29, 1974</div></div></div>\n<p>Far from being an uneventful cruise, <i>Mariner 10</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s three-month journey to Venus was fraught with technical malfunctions, which kept mission control on edge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Donna_Shirley" title="Donna Shirley">Donna Shirley</a> recounted her team&#8217;s frustration: "It seemed as if we were always just patching Mariner 10 together long enough to get it on to the next phase and next crisis."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> A trajectory correction maneuver was made on November 13, 1973. Immediately afterwards, the star-tracker locked onto a bright flake of paint which had come off the spacecraft and lost tracking on the guide star <a href="/wiki/Canopus" title="Canopus">Canopus</a>. An automated safety protocol recovered Canopus, but the problem of flaking paint recurred throughout the mission. The on-board computer also experienced unscheduled resets occasionally, which necessitated reconfiguring the clock sequence and subsystems. Periodic problems with the high-gain antenna also occurred during the cruise. On January 8, a malfunction thought to be caused by a short-circuited diode occurred in the power subsystem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paul_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Paul-14">[14]</a></sup> As a result, the main booster regulator and inverter failed, leaving the spacecraft dependent on the redundant regulator. Mission planners feared that the same problem could recur in the redundant system and cripple the spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 1974, <i>Mariner 10</i> made ultraviolet observations of <a href="/wiki/Comet_Kohoutek" title="Comet Kohoutek">Comet Kohoutek</a>. Another mid-course correction was made on January 21, 1974.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Venus_flyby">Venus flyby</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mariner_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Venus flyby">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The spacecraft passed Venus on February 5, 1974, the closest approach being 5,768&#160;km at 17:01 UT. It was the twelfth spacecraft to reach Venus and the eighth to return data from the planet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> as well as the first mission to succeed in broadcasting images of Venus back to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ulivi181_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ulivi181-61">[61]</a></sup> <i>Mariner 10</i> built upon observations made by <i>Mariner 5</i> six years earlier; importantly, <i>Mariner 10</i> had a camera whereas the prior mission lacked one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reeves_1994,_pp._244_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reeves_1994,_pp._244-62">[62]</a></sup> As <i>Mariner 10</i> veered around Venus, from the planet\'s night side to daylight, the cameras snapped the probe\'s first image of Venus, showing an illuminated arc of clouds over the north pole emerging from darkness. Engineers initially feared that the star-tracker could mistake the much brighter Venus for Canopus, repeating the mishaps with flaking paint. However, the star-tracker did not malfunction. Earth occultation occurred between 17:07 UT and 17:11 UT, during which the spacecraft transmitted X-band radio waves through Venus\' atmosphere, gathering data on cloud structure and temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Although Venus\'s cloud cover is nearly featureless in visible light, it was discovered that extensive cloud detail could be seen through Mariner\'s ultraviolet camera filters. Earth-based ultra-violet observation had shown some indistinct blotching even before <i>Mariner 10</i>, but the detail seen by Mariner was a surprise to most researchers. The probe continued photographing Venus until February 13.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Among the encounter\'s 4,165 acquired photographs, one resulting series of images captured a thick and distinctly patterned atmosphere making a full revolution every four days,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reeves_1994,_pp._244_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reeves_1994,_pp._244-62">[62]</a></sup> just as terrestrial observations had suggested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The mission revealed the composition and meteorological nature of Venus&#8217; atmosphere. Data from the radio science experiment measured the extent to which radio waves passing through the atmosphere were refracted, which was used to calculate the density, pressure, and temperature of the atmosphere at any given altitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howard_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Howard-67">[67]</a></sup> Overall, atmospheric temperature is higher closer to the planet&#8217;s surface, but <i>Mariner 10</i> found four altitudes where the pattern was reversed, which could signify the presence of a layer of clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DB65_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-DB65-68">[68]</a></sup> The inversions occurred at the 56, 61, 63, and 81&#160;km levels,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> confirming previous observations made by the <i>Mariner 5</i> encounter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howard_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Howard-67">[67]</a></sup> The ultraviolet spectrometers identified the chemical substances that comprise Venus&#8217; atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The elevated concentration of atomic oxygen in the upper atmosphere showed that the atmosphere is stratified into upper and lower layers that do not mix with each other; photographs of the upper and lower cloud layers corroborated this hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DB65_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-DB65-68">[68]</a></sup> <i>Mariner 10</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s ultraviolet photographs were an invaluable information source for studying the churning clouds of Venus&#8217; atmosphere. The mission researchers believed the cloud features they photographed were located in the stratosphere and upper troposphere, created by condensation; they also concluded that the contrast between darker and lighter features was due to differences in the cloud&#8217;s absorptivity of UV light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray-Belton_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murray-Belton-71">[71]</a></sup> The subsolar region was of particular interest: as the sun is straight overhead, it imparts more solar energy to this area than other part of the planet. Compared to the rest of the planet&#8217;s atmosphere, the subsolar region was highly active and irregular. "Cells" of air lifted by convection, each up to 500&#160;km wide, were observed forming and dissipating within the span of a few hours; some had polygonal outlines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray-Belton_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Murray-Belton-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The gravity assist was also a success, coming well within the acceptable margin for error. In the four hours between 16:00 UT and 20:00 UT on February 5, <i>Mariner 10</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s heliocentric velocity dropped from 82,785&#160;mph to 72,215&#160;mph.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-No._18_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-No._18-72">[72]</a></sup> This changed the shape of the spacecraft\'s elliptical orbit around the Sun,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ulivi181_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ulivi181-61">[61]</a></sup> so that the perihelion now coincided with the orbit of Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-No._18_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-No._18-72">[72]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:27px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Venus_(diagram).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="346" data-file-width="431" decoding="async" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e9/Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Venus_%28diagram%29.jpg/120px-Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Venus_%28diagram%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e9/Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Venus_%28diagram%29.jpg/180px-Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Venus_%28diagram%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e9/Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Venus_%28diagram%29.jpg/240px-Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Venus_%28diagram%29.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Venus encounter\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus-real_color.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Venus-real_color.jpg/120px-Venus-real_color.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Venus-real_color.jpg/180px-Venus-real_color.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Venus-real_color.jpg/240px-Venus-real_color.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Venus in real colors, processed from clear and blue filtered <i>Mariner 10</i> images\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:28px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus_as_captured_by_Mariner_10.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="2947" decoding="async" height="94" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Venus_as_captured_by_Mariner_10.jpg/120px-Venus_as_captured_by_Mariner_10.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Venus_as_captured_by_Mariner_10.jpg/180px-Venus_as_captured_by_Mariner_10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Venus_as_captured_by_Mariner_10.jpg/240px-Venus_as_captured_by_Mariner_10.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><i>Mariner 10</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s photograph of Venus in <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> light (photo color-enhanced to simulate Venus\'s natural color as the human eye would see it)\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Mercury_flyby">First Mercury flyby</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mariner_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: First Mercury flyby">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The spacecraft flew past Mercury three times. The first Mercury encounter took place at 20:47 <a href="/wiki/Universal_Time" title="Universal Time">UT</a> on March 29, 1974, at a range of 703 kilometers (437&#160;mi), passing on the shadow side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mission_Overview_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mission_Overview-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:51px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Mercury_(diagram).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="396" data-file-width="999" decoding="async" height="48" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/34/Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Mercury_%28diagram%29.jpg/120px-Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Mercury_%28diagram%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/34/Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Mercury_%28diagram%29.jpg/180px-Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Mercury_%28diagram%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/34/Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Mercury_%28diagram%29.jpg/240px-Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Mercury_%28diagram%29.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>First Mercury encounter\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3300" data-file-width="2158" decoding="async" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mercury.jpg/157px-Mercury.jpg 2x" width="78"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Six hours before closest approach\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Mercury_%28outbound_view%29.jpg/160px-Mercury_%28outbound_view%29.jpg 2x" width="80"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Six hours after closest approach\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_Mercury_flyby">Second Mercury flyby</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mariner_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Second Mercury flyby">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>After looping once around the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> while Mercury completed two orbits, <i>Mariner 10</i> flew by Mercury again on September 21, 1974, at a more distant range of 48,069&#160;km (29,869&#160;mi) below the southern hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mission_Overview_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mission_Overview-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:51px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Mercury_(diagram).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="396" data-file-width="999" decoding="async" height="48" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/34/Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Mercury_%28diagram%29.jpg/120px-Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Mercury_%28diagram%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/34/Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Mercury_%28diagram%29.jpg/180px-Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Mercury_%28diagram%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/34/Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Mercury_%28diagram%29.jpg/240px-Mariner_10%27s_encounter_with_Mercury_%28diagram%29.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Second Mercury encounter\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:29.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA03101_Mercury%27s_Southern_Hemisphere.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2086" data-file-width="2743" decoding="async" height="91" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/PIA03101_Mercury%27s_Southern_Hemisphere.jpg/120px-PIA03101_Mercury%27s_Southern_Hemisphere.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/PIA03101_Mercury%27s_Southern_Hemisphere.jpg/180px-PIA03101_Mercury%27s_Southern_Hemisphere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/PIA03101_Mercury%27s_Southern_Hemisphere.jpg/240px-PIA03101_Mercury%27s_Southern_Hemisphere.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Mosaic of images from the second encounter, covering the equator to the south pole\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_Mercury_flyby">Third Mercury flyby</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mariner_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Third Mercury flyby">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>After losing roll control in October 1974, a third and final encounter, the closest to Mercury, took place on March 16, 1975,  at a range of 327&#160;km (203&#160;mi), passing almost over the north pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mission_Overview_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mission_Overview-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mariner_10%27s_third_encounter_with_Mercury_(diagram).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="444" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Mariner_10%27s_third_encounter_with_Mercury_%28diagram%29.jpg/108px-Mariner_10%27s_third_encounter_with_Mercury_%28diagram%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Mariner_10%27s_third_encounter_with_Mercury_%28diagram%29.jpg/162px-Mariner_10%27s_third_encounter_with_Mercury_%28diagram%29.jpg 1.5x, href="/wiki/List_of_albedo_features_on_Mercury" title="List of albedo features on Mercury">Old basin</a>, 190&#160;km in diameter, filled by smooth plains. The basin\'s hummocky rim is partly degraded and cratered by later events\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_mission">End of mission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mariner_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: End of mission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>With its maneuvering gas just about exhausted, <i>Mariner 10</i> started another orbit of the Sun. Engineering tests were continued until March 24, 1975,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mission_Overview_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-Mission_Overview-3">[3]</a></sup>  when final depletion of the nitrogen supply was signaled by the onset of an un-programmed pitch turn. Commands were sent immediately to the spacecraft to turn off its transmitter, and radio signals to Earth ceased.\n</p><p><i>Mariner 10</i> is presumably <a href="/wiki/Heliocentric_orbit" title="Heliocentric orbit">still orbiting the Sun</a>, although its electronics have probably been damaged by the Sun\'s radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> <i>Mariner 10</i> has not been spotted or tracked from Earth since it stopped transmitting. The only way it would not be orbiting would be if it had been hit by an asteroid or gravitationally perturbed by a close encounter with a large body.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discoveries">Discoveries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mariner_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Discoveries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>During its flyby of Venus, <i>Mariner 10</i> discovered evidence of rotating clouds and a very weak magnetic field. Using a near-<a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> filter, it photographed Venus\'s <a href="/wiki/Cirrus_uncinus_cloud" title="Cirrus uncinus cloud">chevron clouds</a> and performed other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_body%27s_atmosphere" title="Celestial body\'s atmosphere">atmospheric</a> studies.\n</p><p>The spacecraft flew past Mercury three times. Owing to the geometry of its orbit &#8211; its orbital period was almost exactly twice Mercury\'s &#8211; the same side of Mercury was sunlit each time, so it was only able to <a href="/wiki/Map" title="Map">map</a> 40&#8211;45% of Mercury&#8217;s surface, taking over 2,800 photos. It revealed a more or less Moon-like surface. It thus contributed enormously to our understanding of Mercury, whose surface had not been successfully resolved through telescopic observation. The regions mapped included most or all of the Shakespeare, Beethoven, Kuiper, Michelangelo, Tolstoj, and Discovery <a href="/wiki/List_of_quadrangles_on_Mercury" title="List of quadrangles on Mercury">quadrangles</a>, half of Bach and Victoria quadrangles, and small portions of Solitudo Persephones (later Neruda), Liguria (later Raditladi), and Borealis quadrangles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h8.pdf_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-h8.pdf-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Mariner 10</i> also discovered that Mercury has a tenuous <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mercury" title="Atmosphere of Mercury">atmosphere</a> consisting primarily of <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, as well as a <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> and a large iron-rich <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">core</a>.\nIts radiometer readings suggested that Mercury has a night time temperature of &#8722;183&#160;&#176;C (&#8722;297 <a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">&#176;F</a>) and maximum daytime temperatures of 187&#160;&#176;C (369&#160;&#176;F).\n</p><p>Planning for <i><a href="/wiki/MESSENGER" title="MESSENGER">MESSENGER</a></i>, a spacecraft that surveyed Mercury until 2015, relied extensively on data and information collected by <i>Mariner 10</i>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mariner_10_1975_Issue-10c.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="575" data-file-width="901" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Mariner_10_1975_Issue-10c.jpg/220px-Mariner_10_1975_Issue-10c.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Mariner_10_1975_Issue-10c.jpg/330px-Mariner_10_1975_Issue-10c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Mariner_10_1975_Issue-10c.jpg/440px-Mariner_10_1975_Issue-10c.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mariner_10_1975_Issue-10c.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Mariner 10</i> Space probe, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Space_Exploration_History_on_U.S._Stamps" title="U.S. Space Exploration History on U.S. Stamps">Issue of 1975</a></div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mariner_10_Commemoration"><i>Mariner 10</i> Commemoration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mariner_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Mariner 10 Commemoration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In 1975, the US Post Office issued a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Space_Exploration_History_on_U.S._Stamps" title="U.S. Space Exploration History on U.S. Stamps">commemorative stamp</a> featuring the <i>Mariner 10</i> space probe. The 10-cent <i>Mariner 10</i> commemorative stamp was issued on April 4, 1975, at Pasadena, California.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since the backup spacecraft was never launched, it was put on exhibition at the <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">National Air and Space Museum</a> of the Smithsonian Institution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mariner_10&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul
